THE City Front Federation at
its meeting last night ordered
a general strike on the docks
of San Francisco, Oakland,
Mission Bock and Port Costa
and in the city of San Francisco, to
gt> into effect this morning'. The
ection of the Federation was prompt
ly ratified amid great enthusiasm by
those unions most directly interested,
and sailors, longshoremen, firemen,
porters and packers, ship clerks and
ether men whose unions are affiliated
"with the organization which took the
initiative Vill not report for work
this morning.
The tie-up will be complete if the
plans of the labor unions are success
ful. They hope to stop the loading
end even to prevent the moving of
chips that are now almost ready for
departure. Unless the proprietors
and managers are able to secure suf
ficient non-union help to load and
man their vessels, the strike will
practically mean the closing of the
port and the suspension of business
in the wholesale and manufacturing
section of San Francisco and Oak
land.
The ferryboats are not affected by
the strike. Union men employed on
them will not be called out, and local
travel and freight transfer 'will not
be disturbed.
The membership of the unions af
fected exceeds 16,000 men. N"ot
that many are employed at all times
but the total number that will b?
cut of employment as the result of
the action of the City Front Federa
tion will not be far from 15,000.
The decision reached by the Federation
was not arrived at until nearly midnight
and then only after long discussion over
a plan of conciliation presented tothrrn.
The final result, however, was at no time
In doubt, for the employers had made no
advances and offered no concessions wor
thy of the name so far as the point of
view of the workingmen was concerned,
and the discussion was not so much on
the central fact of calling out the men on
the docks as on the Cetalls of the pro
posed strike. The proposition to include
Sacramento and Benida was rejected
upon the ground that the war was against
San Francisco employers alone. The
adoption of the resolution declaring the
Btrike was received with enthusiastic
cheers by the delegates' present. The vote
was unanimous, not a delegate from any
union uttering: a protest. Immediately
upon the decision being reached the meet
ing broke up and delegates hurried to
their own unions, where large gatherings
waited Impatiently the news of the. action
of the central body. In each of theseâ the
Bailors' Union, the four Longshoremen's
"Locals" and teamsters in particular â
the announcement of the action of the
Federation was a signal for prolonged
cheering. In all of them ratification of
the action was a foregone conclusion, and
was practically unanimous everywhere.
The following statement of its side of
the case was given to the press by the
City Front Federation:
The City Front Federation was or
ganized for the purpose of bringing
about closer affiliation among the
different organizations connected with
the shipping of this port with the view
of reaching better and more amicable
relations between the employers . and
employes directly interested In the ma
rine transportation of San Francisco.
This policy of the federation has been
strictly adhered to, and it has been in
strumental in preserving peace, where
a less conservative -course would un
doubtedly have precipitated strikes
and disputes detrimental to San Fran
cisco. -;.., '.-'.'

Day of Keen Excitement.
The day has been one of keen excite
ment, replete with interesting scenes. It
is estimated that fully 25,000 persons wit
nessed the drawing. The Immense throng
â¢was wrought up to the highest pitch. The
drawing of the first few names was fol
lowed by a mighty* shout that reverber
ated between the hills and must have
been heard for miles over the prairies.
Each succeeding winning for a time was
met with shouts of applause and merri
ment. All was pleasantry. Every man,
though he did not draw a prize from the
wheels to-day, had steadfast faith that
to-morrow or next day will surely see him
the possessor of a slip reading him a
title clear to lÂ£0 acres of Oklahoma's land.
So in the success of friends or relatives
unfortunate ones, to-day. Instead of be
wailing, their fate, cheered lustily when
familiar names were called out from the
commissioners* platform." '
The crowds fairly exhausted themselves,
and when the close of the drawings for
the day was announced. at 6 o'clock hun
dreds who had neither eaten nor drunk
during the day sank to the ground where
they stood from sheer fatigue or dragged
themselves to better places of rest, or to
refreshment booths uptown. The day was
remarkably free from. quarrels and gen
eral satisfaction with , the . Government's

JACKSON DEFEATS HANDLER.
Colored Lad From California Makes
, Good Showing at Baltimore.
BALTIMORE, July 29.â "Young"' Peter
Jackson, the California colored lad, won
from "Jimmy" Handler ,of Newark, N.
J., to-night In the ninth : round of the
fiercest slugging match ever held in
Baltimore. The contest, which was to
have gone twenty rounds, was held at
Ford's Opera-house under the auspices
of the Eureka Athletic Club, with the
mercury in the neighborhood of 100. Both
men were in good condition, but Jackson
had the better of.- it in this- respect and
the fact won him the battle. Handler
ptarted the slugging as soon as the men
reached the center of the ring, keeping
it up all through the mill. He seemed to
be able to hit the negro when and where
he pleased and hit him hard at that, but
the Calif ornian took his punishment
gamely and stood up under it hand
somely. ' . : Â¦: -
Handler seemed to have the fight in
hand in the sixth round, but the gong
saved Peter and gave him another chance
and he fought warily during the seventh
and eighth rounds. Handler was still
punching hard and often when, just as
the ninth was about to end. Jackson
landed a vicious left swing on the white
boy's Jaw, sending him to the floor aha
setting htm . groggy. After this there
were some wild swings, with Handler
gasping and dazed, until the police 'Or
dered the fighting stopped and the referee
decided In Jackson's^ favor.

FRANCISCO CRISPI'S CABJBEB.
Soldier and Statesman Who Became
Italy's Grand Old Kan.
Francisco Crispi, soldier and statesman,
sometimes called the Grand Old Man of
Italy, was born at Rlbera, Sicily, October
4, 1S19, the same year wlfh the late Queen
Victoria. From the time he attained his
majority until within a year or two ago
he was actively engaged In public life,
a He was one of the founders of the New
Italy and afterward one of the foremost
In perfecting the system of government.
He studied law at Palermo and became a
member of the bar at Naples, where he
engaged â¢ in the conspiracies - that finally
led to the overthrow of the kingdom of
the Two Sicilies in 1848. He was one of
the chief promoters of the insurrection
of Palermo, became a deputy and Grand
Secretary of War, and for two year3 was
the head and heart of the resistance of
fered by the Sicilian people. ' On the vic
tory of the Swiss regiments he fled to
France.
In 1859 . and I860' he , organized- a new
Sicilian revolution, larioed at .Palermo
with Garibaldi and his volunteers and
after fighting as a simple soldier became
a Minister, in which capacity he paved
the â¢ way for the annexation of the Two
Sicilies to the kingdom of Italy.
In 1861 he was returned by the city of
Palermo to the first Italian Parliament
and was thenceforth connected with the
affairs of the nation. He took a most
prominent position and in a short time
became the acknowledged leader of the
Constitutional opposition. He. was very
popular and in 1876 was chosen as deputy
by several electorates at the same time
and was elected president of the Chamber
of Deputies by a vote of 232 to 115.
In 1877. "when, the return of the party
of "Moral Order" to power In France
menaced the interests of Italy. Crispi
visited â¢ London and Berlin in search of
allies and was well received in both
capitals. Soon after he became Minister
of the Interior in the Deputies Cabinet,
but retired after some months' service.
For the next ten years he was the leader
and one of the principal orators of the
In 1S80 he began the movement to make
Italy more prominent in the concert of
nations and to make the Adriatic States,
then under Austrian sway, a part of
Italy
In'' 1885 he declared war against the
Clerical party as being hostile to modern
Italian institutions. After the Italian re
verses at Massowah, in Africa, in 1887,
when the Depritis Cabinet was or
ganized. Crispi became Minister of For
eign Affairs and on the death of that
statesman succeeded him as president of
On October 1 be paid^ the ' first of a

Superior Judge Hart Hakes End of
the Case of Mullenney in
Sacramento.
: SACRAMENTO, July 29.â The sensation
al feature of the work of the Grand Jury
which was recently in session was its in
vestigation.into the acts of City Surveyor
William Mullenney, resulting in an indict
ment charging him. with, malfeasance in
office. Mullenney was accused . of felony
under this Indictment â¢ in having, as al
leged, charged the city -twice -for labor
performed.' This afternoon Superior Judge
Hart dismissed the , Indictment against
Mullenney and ordered his bondsmen re
leased. ' , â¢ â¢ . â¢ -
Various grounds are. given by the court
for this action; among them being the un
warranted action of the Grand Jury dur
ing the investigation In calling in counsel
other than the District Attorney; in al
lowing to be present persons not .author
ized by law to be in attendance; In failing
to secure the required number of Jurors
to vote affirmatively on the indictment,
and in forcing the defendant against his
wish and consent to appear before the
Grand Jury respecting the subject matter
of the indictment. *

Register at Once.
Workingmen, business men, taxpayers
and other good citizens should keep in
mind that registration for the primary
election closes next Saturday, August 3.
Every citizen who has changed his place
of residence since the last election should
re-reglster before August 3. Every man
who has gained the right to vote by re
siding in the State during the past year
should register at once. Young men who
have attained the age of 21 since the last
election should get on the register to vote
at the primary. Registration Is now tho
Important topic.
Address to the Voters.
In view of the importance of the ap
proaching election the Republican Pri
mary League last night issued the follow
ing address to the voters of San Fran
clsco:
The Republican Primary League desires'
to call the attention of the voters to the
fact that the registration for the primary
election will close on August 3, 1901.
The people of San Francisco should realize
that this election will determine the char
acter of the men who will control the
municipal government for the next two
years. While the citizen may be Indiffer
ent at the time of the primary election, he
is not Indifferent when he goes to pay his
tax bill. Good citizens are prone to com
plain of the complexion of political affairs
and vet, when they have the remedy In
their own hands they fall to use it. Under
the new primary law, primary elections are
now under the control of the Election Com
missioners, and every vote cast . at the
primary election will be honestly counted.
The delegates who are to be elected will
meet in* convention and nominate men who
will conduct municipal affairs during the
next two years. It Is fair to presume that

owl and overland from San Francisco
were delayed several hours, but most of
the passengers made their way to neigh

that way. No one was Â¦ injured in the

EXPLAINED TO DELEGATES
PACIFIC GROVE. July 29.â The Sun
day-closing ordinance which was passed
at the last meeting of the Pacific Grove
City Council went into effect yesterday
end caused consternation throughout the
town. The ordinance closes all places of
business within the city limits on Sunday
except drug stores, restaurants, barber
shops, livery stables and transfer and
newspaper agencies. Druggists are pro
hibited from selling anything except-drugs
for medicinal purposes only, and restau
rants are required to close their doors
except during meal hours designated in
the ordinance. Bakery and milk wagons
are prohibited from running during the
day.
The ordinance was rigidly enforced yes
terday, many persons having to go with
out breakfast because they arrived at
their restaurants too late, and others who
neglected to lay in their Sunday supply of
bread on Saturday went breadless all day.
Not a cigar or package of chewing gum
even was sold in the town during the day.
and discontented murmurings were heard
all over town In consequence. It is be
lieved by many that the new ordinance
will cause trouble very soon and many
who do not wish such strict Sunday clos
ing declare it is unconstitutional as well
as a detriment to the growth of the town.
It Is stated that the city fathers are pre
pared to back up its enforcement and
many persons on both sides of the con
troversy predict a bitter fight before the
matter* Is finally settled.
New Ordinance at Pacific Grove
Creates Consternation Among
Some of the People.
STOBES ABE CLOSED

"Four of us, P. J. Rooney of Seattle,
C. Sullivan and F. Sullivan, brothers,
from Butte, Mont., and myself, landed
at Cape Lipin, Unimak Island, on May
12 to prospect the country. On June 7
we landed in a dory at another part of
the island. On returning to the boat at
2 p. m. we found that the Indians had
stolen our guns and ammunition and saw
them hiding behind rocks. They
opened fire without a word of warning
and shot Florence Sullivan dead. They
fired again, hitting Rooney, who fell
mortallv wounded, whereupon an Indian
ran up "to him and shot him dead. An
other shot went through my clothes but
I distanced the natives without sustain
ing further injury.
"I started on foot for False Pass and
on June 13 I reached the pass almost dead
from hunger, exhaustion and pain. Final
ly a prospector discovered me and helped
me to his tent and nursed me back to
health again. The Pacific Steam "Whaling
Company's steamer Newport came along
and took us both to Unalaska."

CLOVERDALE, July 29.â The large ho
tel and numerous cottages at the Geysers
narrowly escaped destruction by fire late
this afternoon. The excellent service ren
dered by the guests In fighting back the
blaze was all that prevented a repetition
of the disaster that befell the Byron
Springs establishment a few days ago.
While the large . number of guests were
at supper about 5 o'clock they were star
tled by a cry of fire from hotel attendants,
who discovered smoke and flame issuing
from the roof. Men, women and children
sprang from their seats at the table, and
while nearly all. were content to gain the
most available exit some of the more
venturesome sought to reach their rooms
with a frantic purpose of saving their
valuables.
The blaze, which had been started by a
defective electric light wire, burned rap
idly through the dry roofing and had
reached alarming proportions when L.
Schoenfeldt and William J. Gallagher of
San Francisco hurriedly organized a hose
and bucket brigade. Every hotel guest
able to bear the burden of a water bucket
was hustled into line, ladders were raised
and after a half-hour of wearying labor
the progress of the fire was arrested. A
number of men clambered upon the bias
ing roof and with axes tore away the
shingles and upper timbers. The damage
to the building is not great and will not
Interfere with the business of. the hotel.

wherever large sums were not paid them

tered Into an ngreement with the French

ern frontier of Tonkin and was paid large

flrhtlnjr trim.

quickly co-operatod with Marnhn.1 Li's
force, with the result that the briprnnrls

TUCSON, Ariz., July 29.â Work ' trains
and bridge crews'are being rushed to the
scene of the -washout . which .began at
Fairbank on the New Mexico and Arizona
Railroad and extended south for many
miles. Altogether ten miles of track has
been washed ; away. One . bridge â¢ 400 feet
long and another 300 feet long were.car
ried away arid an immense grade* which
rose to meet the height of- these big
bridges was completely washed away_by
the flood of water which came down San
Pedro Valley.
The first damage was done several days
ago and as the crews were busily engaged
repairing 'the road another storm set in
and the water shed of the mountains sent
down torrents which overflowed the valley
and rose above the highest grade of track.
The Southern Pacific engineers in Tucson
estimate that at least three days will be
required to repair damage so that the
first train can pass. Passengers from
Mexico are detained at Huachuca siding,
where they are being cared for by the
Southern Pacific Company. '
The flood also washed out a portion of
the Arizona and Southeastern, which runs
from Benson, on the Southern Pacific, to
Bisbee. the big copper camp. The road runs
through the San Pedro Valley and it has
suffered a great deal of damage. Bisbee
Is shut off from the outside world for. a
few, days. It. was reported to-night that
the V people of ' Fairbank are excited over
the rise in the river and are fleeing to the
mountains for safety, since another storm
has set In and it is feared the water will
rise to-night. .Fairbank has twice been
washed away by floods and many people
have been drowned in . the valley near
there. The country for miles below Fair
bank Is under water. Considerable loss of
stock is reported and some damage ' to
buildings in the valley. No lives have thus
far been reported lost.

>rf^ACOMA, July 29.-L. de Lobel, an
I eminent French mining engineer.
1 left last night for Dawson, which
, IL city he will reach twenty days
. . out from Paris, thus making a
record trip.
De Lobel declares he is working on an
Immense railroad" project Involving the
construction of a trans-Alaskan railroad
from Circle City to Bering Sea, across the
ocean by means of mammoth ferries to
Siberia and from the coast by a wide loop
to Vladivostok, where connections- will
be made with, the Trans-Siberian road.
The enterprise will, if it Is put through,
have a capitalization of $300,000,000. The
entire length of the line as planned will
be almost 4000 miles and its construction
will be one of the engineering feats of the
a^Tne undertaking Is said tohave a;
financial backers the Bank of France and
other great moneyed Interests of that
country, as well as of America. It will be
essentially a French-Amerlcan-RuMlan
enterprise and the United States Govern
ment will be asked for concessions in the
shape of railroad lands. ..Â»Â¦?

Circle City to Bering Sea will bejibout
2000 miles, and from the coast of Siberia
to Vladivostok 1800 miles. Its course

Cresceus Coming- to the Coast.
TOLEDO, July 29.â George ' Ketchum,
owner, and driver of Cresceus* the world's
champion trotter, announced to-day, that
he would 6pend next winter on the Pa
cific Coast, where the famous horse will
give speed exhibitions. He will stop at
Denver, Salt Lake City, Sacramento, San
Francisco and Los Angeles.
Speaking of the race with The Abbot,
scheduled for August 13. Ketchum said
1t had not as yet been declared : off .' but
Tne Abbot had been sick and he did not
believe Scanlan would let him go unless
he was in the best of condition.
Miss Isella II. Van Pelt and Thomas
Mills, a banker of Kingston, Canada, were
married yesterOay at noon at the home of
the bride's father, William Van Pelt. 1910
Djevlsadero street It was a quiet wedding
and only a limited number of intimate
friends of the bride witnessed tho cere
mony, which was performed by the Rev.
John Hemphill.
The bride was attended by Miss Nea
mata V. Van Pelt, who officiated as maid
of honor. Charles M. Bufford o nictated
as best man.
Although the wedding was a simple one
the house was prettily decorated for the
happy occasion. Carnations and roses of
every color were generously used for dec
orative purposes.
After the ceremony a bridal breakfast
was served, and then, the happy couple
left for a brief visit to Pacific Grove,
where the honeymoon will be spent. Their
future home will be in Kingston. Canada,
The fair young bride is well known in
musical circles. She is the contralto of
Calvary Church and a member of the
Colonial Ladies' Quartet.

bray of the Frultvale Congregational

future home at Dunsmulr, where Mr. Bal
lard occupies the responsible position of
foreman of the railroad yard. Mrs. Bal
lard was formerly a telephone girl in the
Oakland office.

Charlotte Thompson's "The Adventures
of Nell Gwynne" was reproduced last
night at the Alcazar with Florence Rob
erts as Saucy Nell, the King's sweetheart,

plays Miss Roberts' lighter vein of acting'
at its best, and the character loses noth
ing of its dare-devil features in her por
trayal. Miss Roberts was the character
to the life and earned all the liberal ap
plause that she received.
Her support was exceptionally good.
White Whlttlesey as Charles II was at his
best and proved to be a right royal jeal
ous lover. George P. Webster , was well
cast, the role of Actor Haynes fitting nls
hyperbolic style nicely. The support in
general was excellent.

his ability Is wasted on the role. The

and' Pearl Landers was buoyant as Josle
Armstrong, the "girl who coins words."
Mary Van Buren, as the susceptible,
widow, Mrs. Hillary, lacked finesse and
delicacy. The support was fair.

AND now begins the serious busi
ness of life. Grand opera is upon
us! The summer Is done, the sea
son begun and as serious farewell
as we' ever take of the rosy god
dess-must now be said. "The Babes in
the .Wood" have gone the way of all light
and lonely nonsense, and "Aida," grave
and gracious, now craves a hearing. TEe
only Ferris has. disappeared in the wilds
of Portland, and where he opened, that
abundant mouth of his Salassa now
stalks supreme. Collamarihi rules where
Annie Myers once delighted, and only
change is eternal. Â¦
But there's a deal to be grateful for In
this change. Last night was by much the
best opening night I have ever seen dur
ing my Tivoli experience of grand opera.
Things went with astonishing smooth
ness. The chorus and the orchestral work
were of unusual excellence, and the cast
was beyond comparison tne most satisfac
tory that has been heard in the opera at
the popular house. As to the audience, it
was the usual comfortable, extravagantly
enthusiastic overflowing crowd that in
variably greets the opening opera of the
Tlvoll season, and it outdid its customary
exploits In both appreciation and ap
plause. Each singer got a huge welcome
on his or her appearanceâ first Signor
Dado and Castellano, then Barbareschl
and CoUamarini, then Salassa, then Nico
lini and even little Plo Facci, not to speak
of Mr. Paul Steindorff, the leader. Flow
ers galore there were, too, mostly for the
charming Barbareschl, who won the house
by her artless pleasure In receiving them.
Salassa, too, received many more flowers
than he owned up to, and Collamarlui,
Paul Steindorff and every one was thus
greeted. Altogether, the season has
opened to the tune of success.
Signor Dado Is the single newcomer In
the cast. He made an excellent impres
sion on his first (and only) appearance
here with Madame Sembrlch, and last
night fully Justified the warmth of his
reception. He reminds, in personal ap
pearance, of Edouard de Reszke, and has
a smooth, resonant and deep voice,
used in an absolutely legitimate
fashion. His Ramphis met all re
quirements of the part. Salassaâ what a
welcome he got!â is in excellent voice.
One trembled, remembering his last year s
indisposition, but his opening notes dis
posed of any fear on that score. His
Amonasro is a splendid piece of work
from every standpoint. He looks, sings
and acts the part exactly as it should be
Coilamarini's Amneris Is new to usâ
there is much of Collamarinl herself that
is new to us this yearâ perhaps forty
pounds, for the shapely Carmen is rapidly
acquiring the time-honored build of the
regulation prima donna. One discerns the
Carmen in Amneris, hears the Carmen ac
cent in her voice, but the singer has the
same charm in the part that made her
Carmen so famous. Her voice is lovely
warm, rich, human, thrilling; used at
times marvelously, but again forced as no
human voice should be forced, just as it
was forced in her Carmen. The part is
splendidly acted, with all the power and
passion that makes the singer's work so
distinctive, and the singer was received
with utmost enthusiasm.
Barbareschi was very nervous at the be
ginning of the evening, but later recovered
herself and gave us a charming Aida.
She looks the part, has an excellent make
upâno common thing with Aidasâ and
give3 a true and graceful conception of
the character. Her voice is both sweet
and sympathetic, and were her technique
surer, Barbareschi would be a very ad
mirable singer. She was greeted warmly
by the audience.
Castellano's Radames is an adequate
rendering. He is sure of himselfâ always
there, and his voice smooth and brilliant â
though lacking wholly in resonance, is
very serviceable. Nlcollnl did the King's
part acceptably and Pio Facie the mes
senger.
Paul Steindorff must be congratulated
on the smoothness and finish of the per
formance. He has proven himself a capa
ble and artistic conductor, one of the
best we have had here. The orchestra
was kept In Its place, the rhythms were
elastic, the balance of tone good and the
chorus work was exceptionally worthy.
The scenes and staging were also of the
best.

Nicolini will appear to-night in "Rlgolet
to," the alternating bill for the week.
BLANCHE PARTINGTON.
California.
Henry Arthur Jones' splendid disserta
tion on marriage the equity of the
sexes done up In most palatable form In
his charming play, "The Case of Rebel
lious Susan," brought the Neill Company
back to the California Theater last night.
It was a splendid performance worthy of
the play and the snug little company.
"Rebellious Susan" is a literary play and
a false move, a wrong tone would spoil
it beyond reparation. However, each
player fitted neatly and well Into his or
her respective part and there was nothing
to mar a performance which Individually
and collectively was excellent.
Edythe Chapman was a thoroughly
lovable, -womanly Susan. She dressed the
part well and played It with a delightful
grace and dainty touch. Julia Dean
astonished even her admirers by her
splendid performance of Mrs. Quesnel. Her
lines so neatly given carried well, but her
delicate, girlish appearance was against
her. It takes considerable Imagination to
picture dainty Julia Dean as a worldly
wise widow. Miss Louise Brownell as
Elaine Shrlmpton was forcible and con
vincing. She was the woman's rights
woman to the life.
"Rebellious Susan" Is a play that gives
the men equal chances with the women.
Sir Richard Kato Is essentially a role that
actors call "fat," and Neill took the
middle of the stage with it and held it in
spite of all comers. John TV. Burton was
a salty Admiral Sir Joseph Darby. George
Bloomquest certainly "left his stamp
upon" the audience as Fergusson Poybus,
and Donald Bowles was dreadfully in
earnest as Lucien Edensor.
The play was splendidly mounted and
should do a splendid week's business.

work of a well-cast company, evoked,

ity has not waned. The play Is an excit
ing one, and it teaches a wholesome, les-
Bon in patriotism and love of flag and
country. These are qualities which the
average American cannot Ignore, and it
partly accounted for the. frequent out
bursts of applause that greeted the vari
ous scenes as presented last night.
James M. Brophy was at his best as
Ensign Ben Baird. his â¢ scene before the
naval .court of inquiry being especially
strong. As the bluff old coxswain, Jack
Dudley, James Corrlgan shared with the
star the honors of the evening. Howard
Scott, an Alcazar favorite, who made : his

trayal of the part of Captain "Wllkes.

RAIN PREVAILS
IN CORN BELT
Weather Bureau Predicts
Showers East of the Mis
souri River.
Reduced.
Temperature in Parched
District Materially
WASHINGTON, July 29.â Official advices
to the Weather Bureau are confirmative
of the press reports of the prevalence of
rains over the corn belt last night, with
cooler weather than yesterday. During
the past twenty-four hours rain has fallen
very generally in that section with some
few exceptions, notably Southern Ohio,
Kentucky and Southwestern Nebraska.
While not very heavy In amount, the
rains are described officially as pretty fair
for summer time. At Kansas City there
was' over an inch of precipitation, from a
quarter to almost two inches in various
parts of Iowa, in Nebraska the rainfall
was fair, in Kastern and Northern Okla
homa there were some showers, but In
Missouri they were, pretty fair. Showers
are predicted to-morrow east of the Mis
souri River and fair weather west. Tem
peratures were generally above 90 degrees,
but in .some localities they did not get so
high. For the next day or two tempera
tures will be reasonably moderate as com
pared with those of the past heated pe
riods Â¦ - f Â¦
In many sections of the Middle Atlantic
States there have been high temperatures
to-day, but in Northern New Jersey,
Pennsylvania and New York they were
reduced to-day as a result of showers. In
Washington the weather has been exceed
ingly oppressive to-day, the thermometer
registering a maximum of 97 degrees, with
an unusual degree of humidity in the at
mosphere.
STOCKTON, July- 29.â Attorney A. &.
Carpenter, who was indicted by the
Grand Jury on charges of perjury and
subornation of perjury, to-day swore to
an affidavit before the County Clerk de
manding a change of venue. The affidavit
states that Judges Budd and Jones are
prejudiced against the affiant and that
he cannot secure a fair trial in thi3 coun
ty. He asks that the case be transferred
to Sacramento County or that Judge "W.
G. Lorlgan of Santa Clara County be
called to preside at the trial.
Says Judges Are Prejudiced.
She Is Not. Doing Herself
Justice and "Will Not
Go to Newport.
COLUMBIA WINS
THE ASTOR CUP
Behavior of Constitution
Proves Exceedingly
Disappointing.
The Boston boat Independence started
over the course about a mile ahead of the
racers. She tackled them to leeward off
the Block Island mark and did windward
work similar to that of the racers, but
at no time did she show any great speed.
Start. Finish. El'ps'd. Cor'ctd.
Constitution Â».ll:07:38 2:56:51 3:49:13 3:49:13
Columbia 11:08:19 2:54:33 3:46:19 3:44:45
Â¦ The Columbia covered this long leg
three minutes and nineteen seconds faster
than the new boat. This settled the race,
for the Constitution was unable to even
cut down this lead on the. short run home
and so they finished. The" summary:
As the two 90-footers rounded the Block
Island mark the Constitution had the
weather berth. She did not keep it long.
Instead of a beat to windward, the beat
to the second mark resolved itself. into
a very long leg on the port tack, with a
couple of short hitches on the starboard
stretch.
It is not easy for the best friend of the
Constitution to make plausible excuses
for her most palpable defeat. She was
beaten fairly and squarely on her merit J
Running almost dead before the wind on
the first leg of the course she at first
opened quite a gap of clear water between
herself and the Columbia, and it looked as
though she would have a long lead in the
run down the wind. So long as the wind
continued light she had it all her own
way, but when it freshened the Columbia
slowly but surely overhauled her and
when they got to the first mark was only
a few lengths astern..
The behavior of the Constitution on the
other^ hand was disappointing: in the ex
treme and this evening- Mr. Duncan an
nounced that his boat would not take- part
in f urther\ races until her rig had been
altered. He declared that the Constitu
tion is not doing herself Justice; that she
is not going well and that there is no uss
in racing her further until she Is put in
proper shape. Â¦ .
The Constitution will not participate In
the Newport- yacht racing events of
Thursday and Saturday of this week as
a competitor^ of ithe Independence. The
Columbia, however, has been entered for
these races and will sail against the Bos
ton boat.- The Constitution will go to
Bristol at once to have the alterations
made. If this change in the plans of the
Constitution does' not prevent, the next
races in which she will start will take
place over the Newport course on August
12 and 14 and the regatta, wiir be under
the auspices of the, New York .Yacht Club."
In; one of. these races the cup. offered by
Sir Thomas Lipton will be sailed for. The
regular- trial races for the purpose of se
lecting the defender of the America's cup
will be held August 31 and September 1
and 3. The wind to-day never exceeded
thirteen knots, and although there was
a long roll of. the sea setting In from the
southwest, ' the water â¢ was by.no means
rough. In fact. Newport catboats took it
quite kindly with a single reef in their
main sails. The course was a total dis
tance of thirty-eight nautical miles. The
wind was north by east at the start and
north-northeast at the finish. It started
with a force of about nine knots when
the preparatory gun was fired and fresh
ening gradually as the race progressed,
it attained a strength of thirteen or four
teen knots, when the Columbia was greet
ed as the winner. " -
NEWPORT, R. I., July 29.â The Colum

for boat in a splendid race by two min
utes and fifty-four seconds and won the

Her victory was decisive and convincing.

BARGE AND STEAMER
COLLIDE IN THE FOG
Former Vessel Was Heavily Loaded
With Iron and Sinks With
Three of the Crew.
SAULTSTE. MARIE. Mich.. July 29.â
The whaleback barge Sagamore, loaded
with iron ore. was sunk in collision with
the Northern line freight steamer North
ern Queen near Point Iroquois to-day dur
ing a fog. Of the crew of eight men two
were drowned and one is missing. The
dead:
CAPTAIN E. JOYNER, master of the
Sagamore, lived at Henderson, N. Y.
IRA IVES. cook on the Sagamore, lived
at Sacketts Harbor, N. Y.
The missing: Hurley Smith, seaman on
the Sagamore, lived at Woodville, N. Y.
Threshing Crews Wreck Saloons.
WICHITA, Kans., July 29.â Eight threan
ing machine crews struck the town of Col
wich, in this county, yesterday, and be
cause they could not get liquor Â¦ owing to
a Sunday law smashed five joints
and in addition wrecked a number of
town pumps. The citizens organized a
party to cause their arrest, but the thresh
ers made them retreat. The rioting was
resumed to-day, when the citizens again
tried to arrest the threshers, but again
they had to give up. The Sheriff has been
asked to come at once to the scene.

Indemnity Question Settled.
CALL- BUREAU, 140G G STREET.
N. W., WASHINGTON, July 29.â Special
Commissioner Rockhill cabled Acting Sec
retary HJ11 to-day that the foreign Min
isters in Peking have : completed the dis
cussion of the indemnity question. Inter
est on the indemnity will be payable seml
annually, Rockhill reports. Interest is to
begin with the 1st of July of this year,
and the first payment is to be made Jan
uary 1 next. In order to give Qhina an
opportunity to secure' a balance in her
treasury she will be given three years for
the payment. of the first .installment on
the principal.. Â¦ . .
-Payments will be made to a financial
committee stationed in Shanghai. The
committee will be a mixed body, cafled
the committee of encashment, and will be
composed of the heads of the foreign
banks at Shanghai designated by the in
terested governments to receive and dis
tribute the money they shall have collect
ed from China. The committee Â¦will have
no other function than receiving and dis
tributing the funds. Â¦Â¦ Â¦ . Â¦ . . â¢

Automobiles as Transport Wagons.
Experiments In France have proved convinc
ing, and the French believe they are certain
to play a role of much importance in modern
warfare. It is odd to note the different uses
to which nature and science are put.- On the
battlefield they fight for the destruction of lire,
while throughout the country, Hostetter's
Stomach Bitters fights to preserve it. For fifty
years the Bitters has been curing dyspepsia, in
digestion, constipation and biliousness. It will
also prevent malaria, fever and ague.
A Financial Success.
At no time In the history of the State has
there been such a determined effort on the part
cf enterprising firms and corporations to bring
to the notice of the consumer the various exÂ»
cellent food products of California as Js now
being made, and the results already obtained
are most gratifying.
The moft marked success is that of the Fig
prune Cereal Company of San Jose. This com
pany be^aa operations Jn a Fmall way last
August and the demand for its product, - Fig
prune Cereal, a delicious cereal coffee, has in
creased with such rapidity that the erection of
a fai-tory large enough to meet the requirements
of the Eastern trade Is necessary.
Plans for a modern plant with a- capacity,
when fully equipped, of one million cases per
year have been prepared and ground for the
ceu- buiJdins w'JI be broken next month.
NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of. occurrence as to time of
day: the third time column gives the last tide
of the day, except when there are but three
tides, as sometimes occurs. The heights given
are in addition to the soundings on the United
States Coast Survey charts, except when a
minus ( â > sign precedes. the height, and then
the number given Is subtracted from the depth
given by. the charts. The plane of reference is
the mean of the lower low waters.

STEAMSHIP SAMOA DISABLED.
Broke Her Shaft While Bound From
Mendocino to San Pedro.
The steam schooner Samoa while bound
from Mendocino for San Pedro broke her
shaft last Saturday night and is now
making her way south under canvas. Sun
day she was spoken by the Iaqua and
offered a tow. but the captain refused,
eaylngr he was doing- very well as it was.
The Iaqua was twenty-five miles south
east of Piedras Blancas when Captain
Gunderson sighted the Samoa. The Sa
moa has been three years In service, and
this Is her first mishap. This is the
fourth trip the Iaqua has made since she
was repaired after having been ashore on
Duxbury Reef.

Admitted to West Point.
WEST POINT. N. T.. July 29.â The fol
lowing candidates who reported last week
successfully passed the examinations an3
were to-day admitted to the Military Aca
demy as cadets: Rupert A.- Dunford,
Utah; Halsey Dun woody, at large;
Charles K. Lyman, Hawaii; Norman F.
Ramsey. Kansas; George R. Rogers, Cali
fornia: Calvin P. Titus, at large.
Forty-eight reported for examination.
Thirty were successful, six failed, two
were rejected by the medical board and
ten alternates passed for whom vacancies
exist.
The number which passed beats all rec
cords. Titus is the young man who was
the first to scale the wall at Peking. Ly
man of Hawaii is the first cadet appointed
from the Government's new possessions.
The corps now numbers 464 cadets, within
eighteen of the full number provided for.
Congressional Committee in Utah.
SALT LAKE. July 29.â The members of
the House Rivers and Hafbors Commit
tee, who are returning to Washington
after an extended inspection of the Pa
cafic Coast, arrived in this city from the
Northwest earjy to-day. During the day
the party was driven around the city,
called upon President Snow of the Mor
mon church, listened to a special recital
on the great ore-an in the Mormon Taber
nacle and visited Saltair. where they
took a dip In the Great Salt Lake. To
night the party resumed its journey east
ward. They w:ll spend two days at Colo
rado Springs, one day in Denver and are
scheduled to arrive in Chicago on Sunday.

North American Commercial
1 Company's steamship Conemaugh
Â¦ arrived from Nome direct yester
1L ' day morning and during the after
noon the St. Paul of the same
company sailed for the gold fields. The
Conemaugh brought down no passengers
and but little freight. From St. Michael
and Nome came $651,000 in gold dust, all
consigned to the owners of the vessel.
There was only one steamship â the Dis
covery â at Nome when the Conemaugh
left and she had been ordered home by
the inspectors of hulls and boilers.
The Conemaugh is a freight boat, and
on her last trip north took up eighty head
of beef cattle, eighty horses, 1000 sheep
and 500 hogs. These were all landed at
Nome in twenty-four hours and the peo
ple got their first taste of fresh meat in
ri'Oiiths the next day. The officers of the
steamship say that Nome and Teller are
overrun with cheap labor, men being
willing to work for their prrub. At St.
Michael men could not be procured to han
dle the freieht and a '.steamer had to be
sent to Nome to bring down laborers.
The Conemaugh brought downline re
mains of the wife'of Colonel H. C. Leacb.
They were taken in charge by an under
taker and will be buried to-day.
The St. Paul took away a very large
general cargo and about thirty passen
gers. Among- those who went away on
the steamship were two brides, one bride
to-be and a lady who was so anxious to
get north that she left all her baggage
behind and sailed for Nome in the clothes
in which she stood. The St. Paul's cabin
passengers were: : :
Mrs. Lancaster and child. Mrs. Muldowney,
M'ss A. Hill. James Hill. Miss M. Hill, Mrs.
John Vhitley. H. L Dixon, S. Carter, Mrs.
S. Carter. Mrs. J. S. McCann. J. D. Will.
Mrs. J. II. Baker. J. S. McCann, Mrs. H.
Turner Miss E. Thomas, W. A. Maxwell, Mrs.
A. Zipf. A. Meyer, "\V. F. Gibson and wife,
F. R. Gibson, H. C. Pasquale, F. B. Mal
donado. Miss S. Elden, Mrs. M. L. Washbura,
Mrs. E. Lundberg and Cora Hepworth.
Mrs. S. Carter Is one of the brides. Her
husband is the agent of the Standard Oil
Company at Nome, and a few days ago
he arrived here to get" married. He is
now returning with his bride> Mrs. ' A.
Zipf is the other bride. She was formerly
Miss Sadie Walsh, the actress, and her
husband is in the employ of the North
American Commercial Company. He went
north on the last steamship, and Â¦ she is
now following him. Miss E. Thomas is
the bride-Jo-be. She is going north to
marry Oliver Marston, a Nome merchant.
Cora Hepworth is the lady who caught
the St. Paul at the last moment. Noth
inpr is known about her save that she
was In a desperate hurry to Â£et north.
She asked that her baggage, be sent after
her on the Conemaugh, and then taking
a hack dashed down to the front and
caught the last boat out to the steam
ship. Should the Conemaugh be tied up
by the strike Miss or Mrs. Hepworth will
have to buy a new outfit at Nome.
Other passengers by the St. Paul were
Mrs Turner, whose husband is employed
by the North American Commercial Com
pany at Nome, and Mrs. M. L. Washburn,
wife of the superintendent of "the same
concern. Mrs. E. Lundberg is going north
to join her husband.
Bark Andrew Welch in Trouble.
The sugar packet Andrew Welch had a
narrow escape from going on Montara
Reef yesterday morning. She made a long
run from Honolulu and when the coast was
reached a dense fog came down. - Then a
dead calm fell and the current took com
mand of the bark. At 7:30 a. m. the cap
tain found that Montara Reef was only
a mile and a half away and that his ves
sel was drifting down on it. Luckily the
steam schooner Alcazar came along and
offered a tow, which was gladly accepted.
The steamer at once put a line aboard and
towed the Andrew Welch Into port.
Water Front Notes.
The British bark Acnashie arrived from
Talcahuano yesterday in ballast. She Is
under charter to load wheat for Europe.
The steamer Ruth has finally reached
Beattle and will be overhauled there.
After being got off the beach at Golofnin
she was towed to Nome by the Santa
Ana, -then the Brunswick took hold and
tewed her to St. Michael and at the latter
place the Buckingham took her In tow
and brousht her to the Sound.
Frank Herbert, a lad of 10, was nearly
drowned yesterday. He was fishing from
Washington-street wharf and, losing his
balance, fell overboard. He was pulled
out by a bystander and sent to the Har
bor Hospital, where his clothes were
dried. Afterward the lad was taken to
his home at 611 Ellis street. â¢ :

CALL. BUREAU. 1406 O STREET. N.
W\, WASHINGTON. July 29.â Until, they
are called by the Dexrey court of inquiry
end make under oath statements of any
information they may possess, officers of
the navy must refrain from publicly dis
cussing the Bampson-Schley controversy.
Secretary Long issued to-day the gen
eral order he decided upon last week as a
result of the discussion then in progress.
This Is the order:
Xavy Department,
Â¦WASHINGTON'. D. C. July 29. 1901.
Geieral- order No. 12â All persons in the naval
service are strictly enjoined to refrain from
any public statement concerning the subject
matter of the court of inquiry requested by
Hear Admiral W. S. Schley.
JOHN B. LONG, Secretary.
TVhile there is a general naval regula
tion which forbids officers from publicly
talking- about matters pertaining to the
personnel of 'he naval service without
express authority from the department,
no attention wis apparently paid to that.
Testifying under oath, the exact truth
will be obtained, and the Secretary be
lieves that thj country would prefer to
wait until it can be thus obtained.
Xo intimation has been received by the
department from Rear Admiral Schley
that he desires any change in the precept
issued by the department relative to the
investigation to be made by the Dewey
court of inquiry. If Rear Admiral. Schley
objects to that provision directing an in
quiry into 'â¢circumstances attending and
reasons for disobedience," of which he is
alleged to be guilty, the Secretary -will
take under consideration the advisability
of making- a change. This specification of
the precept was particularly drawn, and
the department used as a basis for the
language employed Admiral Schley's own
words, "much to be regretted, cannot obey
orders of the .iepartment."

ANOTHER CHICAGO UTTERANCE.
CHICAGO University, under the' potent spell
of the Rockefeller millions, rose as rapidly to
~ eminence as Aladdin's enchanted palace, and
it has been making reputation through the mouths of
its professors about as rapidly as it rose. First came
the announcement of a theory of human valuations,
which one of the professors set forth by way of prov
ing Rockefeller to be greater than Shakespeare. In
quick succession followed manifestos from various
members of the faculty declaring the feasibility of liv
ing on 10 cents a day, the folly of marrying a co-edu
cated woman, the necessity of a continuous stream of
immigration from Europe to prevent the American
people from reverting to the Indian type;, and now
comes the further assertion from' the same learned
source that collegesgoing has become'a'fad among
young men and women in this country arid that a ma
jority of students have^no real ambition for learning
and ought not to be at the universities.-
The expense of trying Earl Russell for bigamy be
fore the House of Lords has been so large that there
is talk in Parliament of abolishing the right of a
nobleman to have such a trial, and so another old his
toric privilege of the British aristocracy is-^about to
perish because it does not suit an age where money
counts for more than birth.

One expression of the whist face, though
It is sad, and is told with an effort, is age.
The women may shudder at the thought,
but the fact remains. Intense thought and
daily racking the memory -to remember
what cards have been played' cannot but
deepen the lines and hasten what hereto
fore the women have left for time to ac
complish without their permissionrrM '
. We have all sorts of faces/all varieties
of. countenances, that get their recognized
expressions from close application to some
variety of straining employment. Now we
have , "the whist face" to add to the list.
It is showing itself among the women, and
comes from the whist club habit, which is
rapidly growing, showing its strength not
only at' night, but in the afternoons and
even in the mornings, associated with tea
and wafers, chocolates and bonbons. r
Nature can recover from this sort of
thing when occasionally indulged in, but
when it becomes a habit, practiced almost
daily, and sometimes for two or more ses
sions a day, the expression becomes fixed
and follows the wearer, no matter what
her thought or occupation may be at the
time. ;: vAv.vvl-.
In the whist face you will see the far
away look, accompanied by arched lines
on. the forehead, suddenly- changing to an
expression of inward contemplation, as
sociated with a tightening 1 of the lips.
These expressions come from an acrobatic
mind under training that consists in leap
ing from the first lead, Jump b>vjump,
over each successive play that must be
made, while keeping the mental eye on
the accepted rules, not neglecting to ob
serve and respond to signals.

MOLLY MAGUIRESâ B., City. The or
iginal Molly Maguires were an association
formed in Ireland more than fifty years
ago, "to protect Irish renters against
heartless and"cruel landlords." It is said
that the organization .took Its name front
a woman named Molly Maguire, who had
taken her cause into her own hands when
a process of eviction was served upon her,
and that she successfully resisted the bai
liffs who had been sent to evict her. The
young men who composed the society used
to dress in women's clothing when en
gaged in resisting a bailiff or other offi
cers employed by landlords.

MAZUZAâ F. R. H., City. The little roll
of parchment sent to this department for.
examination is a Mazuza. which is a
prayer used to a very- great extent by the
European Jews to ward off evil. It is
placed in a small tin tube having a small
opening in it and bearing: on its face the
word Shaddal (Almighty). -It la then
nailed at the upper part of the left side
of the main door of- a dwelling.

COMPROMISING SOHLEY'S CASE.
I"Tâ¢1 VER. since the controversy arose between the
r""< partisans of Admiral Sampson and Admiral
Â¦*â ' Schley, the yellow journals have been doing
their best to make the most of it and to gain some
thing of popularity for themselves by posing as the
advocates of Schley. The call for a court of inquiry
to investigate and report upon the charges made
against Schley has given these journals a new oppor
tunity for displaying their activity and making fools of
themselves and they have been prompt . to take
advantage of it.
Already they have set to work urging their readers
to "write to the President" demanding "protection"
for Schley. A more foolish proceeding could not
well be devised. The President has no power over
the court and if he had it would not be proper for
him to interfere in its proceedings. The only stage at
which he can interfere in such matters is that of
pardoning persons who have been convicted. There
fore to write to the President now is tantamount to
an expression of a belief that Schley has been guilty
of some wrong and ought to be pardoned.
Such a misrepresentation of Schley's attitude does
him gross wrong. He himself requested the court of
inquiry. He goes before it to vindicate himself against
aspersions that have been put upon him by his
enemies, and the persons who^are persuaded to write
to the President will injure rather than help Schley,
for they will give his enemies an opportunity to assert
that he is afraid of the very investigation he asked for
and that his friends are trying to help him out by
petitioning the President. *V; .
Another freak in the way of support for Schley
was that of publishing an alleged interview with
Admiral Cervera of the Spanish navy, in which the
Spaniard h reported to have said that Schley is "a
-gallant officer and a high minded gentleman;" and to
have added many other laudatory things. It is
doubtful if any such interview were ever had with
Cervera, but, admitting it were so, what would such
testimony be worth? Does any sane man suppose
""that Cervera, in talking for publication about the
commandeÂ« of the fleet which destroyed his own,
would say anything derogatory to his courage or his
skill? Would Cervera assert he had been conquered
by a coward? ~ m Â¦
Do the yellow journals or do any of their readers
believe that Admiral Schley needs petitions to the
President or the testimony ofSpaniards at this junc
ture? Has he not in the record of his glorious victory
ample proof of his seamanship, his valor, his ability
as a tactician and his loyalty?
Of course there is no way of preventing the yellow
journals from making such use of the occasion as
they deem most advantageous to themselves, but it is
a shame that, a gallant hero who has been assailed
already in so mean a manner by slanderous foes
should, now that he has summoned them to court,
have his ca?e compromised by sensational fakers.
Schley does not need nor do his friends ask for any
such defense as the yellow journals have put forward
for him. His case was well put by his son, Captain
Schley, in the recent stafement:
"When the court of inquiry meets I would like to
have the privilege of asking Admiral Sampson just
one questionâ 'If the battle of Santiago had resulted
in defeat, to whom would the blame have been at
tached?* This. I think, tells the situation in a nut
shell. If Admiral Schley' had lost the battle he would
have been blamed. He won it, and he should have
the credit."
We believe there will be no disputing that proposi
tion. The ''hero of Santiago is the' officer under whose
command the battle was fought and won.' That he
has fiad powerful foes is now evident, but powerful
as they are and grievously as they have injured him
it is doubtful if they have done, him more harm than
the yellow journals will do by their method of posing

A CHANCE TO SMILE.
r 1 land on which John Alexander Dowie, or Elijah 111,
I as he now calls himself, proposes to establish the great
âÂ¦: I urban colony of his followers, to be known as Zion City,
* lies on Lake Michigan, near "Waukegan, Wis. The Chi-
. cago and Northwestern Railroad passes through the sitÂ©
and another road very near it. ,
The stretch of prairie land has been surveyed and laid off in-
blocks! lots and parks, with streets and avenues between. In
the middle is a large square on which it is designed to place the
Temple of Zion
ah tvi^ ...Â»â*,, x- rr, nn *,ââ_ t3iku__i âââ,ââ bv.-jj- - j
?i, s streets. treets ln Zion have Biblical names. Sheridan road
runs through a part of the town site, but Dowie has changed
the name of this to "Elijah avenue." Among the other avenues
are Gabriel, Ezekiel, Elim, Elizabeth, Eden. Ebenezer, Deborah
Bethany and Bethlehem. There are Bethel, Bethesda, Shiloh
ancTSalpm houipvarrl* nnd niimprniK nthpr thnrm^hfaros >mvo
ii Â« boulevards, and numerous other thoroughfares have
appellations familiar in sacred, history. .
On the 15th inst. the great sale of lots, or rather leases, was
Inaugurated and expected to continue eight days. Two hundred
thousand people were expected to attend, but not 3000 put In an
appearance. Three hundred lots were sold, giving- in
to Dowle's hands some $150,000. The leases which are
given the dupes in exchange for their cash are for 1100 years,
but are hampered and surrounded by conditions which make it
a matter of option with Dowie to declare the leasehold vacated
if he sÂ«ffrflt. An added clause provides for a return of E0 per
cent of the value in case of the lease being declared vacant. In
price the lots ranged from $270 to $2700. Some buyers were
guided by the names of the streets rather than the location oC
f ots. Th * nearestfcapproach to a rush for particular sites oc
curred when the advance guard discovered Gabriel avenue. Tha
lots on that thoroughfare were soon taken. Many looked'with
longing eyes on the choice locations along Elijah boulevard, but
few selected lots there. The $2700 lots are located on this street.
Some buyers went to Ezra avenue, others to Ezekiel avenue,
whlle stiirothers were satisfied with Enoch. The lowly follow
ers secured the cheapest lots, which are on the numbered
streets. Biblical names came high.
-At the same time a study of the points of difference
between man and the. lower animals gives probability
to Professor Koch's theory. Some animals are lack
ing in~the organic .machinery, required for some of
the,- functions that arc necessary, to the life of man.
Some animals have no gall, and some are immune to
poisons that are fatal to man, while they succumb to
doses of substances that are harmless to man. The
smallest quantity of saltpeter destroys sheep as
effectually as strychnine destroys man. There must
be, then, important physiological differences between
man and his brother" animals, since there are actual
anatomical differences. Animals* have their materia
medica. A cow will seek ribbon grass when . her
appetite is off, as will "a sheep. All animals' of the
sheep kind eat tobacco when" they can get it, and chew
down. a plug of the strongest with an energy and
appetite which show the craving of a natural need of
it. A dog corrects his disordered digestion by eating
grassland all animals of the cat kind seek catnip. . To
the larger varieties of that family it seems to be more
than a medicine, and is a luxury. Any one who has
seen a big lion go into raptures ov.er a' package of
catnip thrown into his, cage. can easily/realize that it
is to him what pipe and glass are to man. A buzzard
is immune to arsenic and fattens on it. Many other
facts may be quoted to show where man and brute
part company, physiologically, anatomically and path
ologically, and every such fact lends some support to
the theory of Professor Koch. . What is, wanted now
is a man to try it on.'
Unfortunately he is limited for experiment to just
one-half the field to be covered by the inquiry. He
has found it impossible to infect cattle with human
tuberculosis, but has . not found any one willing to
serve as the subject to the complementary experiment
of transmission, of the bovine tuberculosis to man.
He is confident, however, of the curability of con
sumption, and if this be demonstrated it should not
be difficult to find some one willing to take the risk
of. enlightening the world as to its origin, by sub
mitting to inoculation with the bovine variety. One
such experiment would not settle the matter, since
nearly, all men are now equally exposed to inocula
tion with consumption through the germs that are
spread broadcast by its victims, but only a percentage
of those exposed acquire the disease. So, many cross
inoculations will be required to prove that bovine
tuberculosis is innocuous to man.
There seem to be but few features in common
between an epidemic and an epizootic. Man' seems to
be differentiated from his brethren of the lower orders
in the animal kingdom. Animals do not have the
eruptive fevers, such as smallpox, measles and scarlet
fever, though these become epidemic among men.
Nor do animals have the enteric fevers, which are
also epidemic under certain conditions. They.- are
free also from the diseases of the alimentary tract
which afflict man. While "colt distemper" in horses
may be said to be the analogue of what are called the
juvenile diseases in man, it is not truly so, since it is
not common to all young horses; does not appear to
leave immunity behind it, nor to be apt to occur in
later life, if escaped in infancy. The epizootics seem
to be distinct in character from epidemics. The
presence of what has been called bovine tuberculosis
is not accompanied by any of the phenomena which
are present in consumption in man. There is no
suspension of nutrition, no / atrophy. The organic'
functions are not impaired. Thejrow is sleek, fat and
alert. So far as symptoms go there is . ground for
going further than Koch, and declaring that the germ
observed in cattle and called the tubercle is a .benign
parasite, innocuous and beneficial. Of course in a
matter of such vast import to mankind, it is wrong to
take any risks, and there should be no relaxation of
milk inspection until wide experiment has made
Professor Koch's reassuring theory a scientific cer
tainty.
There is a backing of probability to Professor
Koch's opinion, aside from the scientific basis which
a man of his eminence may be expected" to have for a
conclusion of such vast import to the world.
Now comes Professor Koch, the most distinguished
bacteriologist in theÂ»world, and declares to. the Con
sumption Congress' of physicians in London that
bovine tuberculosis is incommunicable to man, and
the precautions taken against it may be called off.
SINCE the discovery of a germ in cattle like the
tubercle in man it. has been taken for granted
that the two. were identical, and that tubercu
losis is communicable from cattle to man. So fixed
has this" opinion become that physicians and veterih
aries regard it as an orthodox conclusion of science,
and general efforts have been made to extirpate
bovine tuberculosis by extermination of affected herds.
PROFESSOR KOCH'S THEORY.
It is a bad wind that blows nobody" good, and
accordingly it is not surprising to learn that .the
suburban hotels around the big cities of the East have
coined money out of the hot spell. It is said that
<rt Coney Island hotels the dining rooms, parlors, and
even the bathrooms have been filled with cots for
sleepers who would not go back to the city.
At last we have reached the line of departure beyond
which there can be no division. The call to duty, is
to the real strenuous life. All should answer it. each
in his way, and there is a way for all. If we fail and
say so manfully we lose no prestige. If we fail and
deny it unmanfully we lose all.
Patience, probity, ,moral and physical courage,
abstention from the sordid use of power, respect for
native simplicity and weakness, must all have place in
our administration. . Denying to them their aspiration
for independence and self-government, we are about
to give them what we believe is a better government
than they can give themselves. Will they show kinship
of spirit with us by being "ungovernable by any
power but themselves?" If they do, what will we do?
Will we treat it as treason and deserving punishment,
or will we have the self-control and sense of justice to
salute it as vindication of their manhood? Ethics in
government is invaded by considerations of national
prestige. Perhaps a majority of men believe with the
German Emperor that the noblest instrument is the
sword. But surely a strong minority believe that the
noblest principle is justice. . ,
Now all this is changed. The notes of the war
bugle are growing fainter. The rush of the charge at
quickstep, the thrill of revenge, the thirst for glory,
have but feeble influence, for they are passing, and as
they pass the promptings of the conscience become
vocal and call this nation to harder duties than war
and to possible victories more glorious than are won
in battle.
It may be said with much justice that all men in
some degree share that quality with us. That spirit
in a people may be destroyed by killing the people
whom it inspires, or by wearing out their physical
power of resistance. \ But it is the spirit that has
enfranchised man wherever he has risen from bond
age. It is with the forlorn Boer when night falls upon
the veldt, and he sleeps on his rifle-stock for a pillow.
It nerved Rizal and Aguinaldo in their defiance of
Spanish power in 1896, and, to do them justice, was
the motive of the Filipinos in the hopeless resistance
to our arms, which made us indifferent to their fate,
while it lasted.
The character of the American people will therein
be tested, and over the most righteous performance
of duty will lie the shadow of man's unrest under the
government of any one but himself. Thomas F.
Bayard, while Embassador to Great Britain, in an
address to Edinburgh University for which he was
foolishly criticized, described the American people as
so stubborn and determined, that "they are ungov
ernable by any power but themselves." That is the
finest description and ascription of our national
character that has ever been uttered.
A similar time is already upon us. The Filipino
story of the war against the natives will never be told,
unless some Kinglake shall appear among us to tell
it. And if he do, it will not touch us as we will be
touched by the incidents that are to come in the
government of the islands.
But we are entering now upon the real strenuous
period, which will test us as war cannot test a nation.
The civil and social reorganization of about twelve
millions of people in the Philippines, provision for
their rights and prosperity without abridging our own,
will call for the exercise of genius and manifestation
of character to a degree far beyond any demand of
war. In fact, war is easy compared to the demands
of peace. England was less troubled by India when
Hastings was setting, the natives by the ears, bribing
or hanging them at will, as suited his purpose, and
Give was rising from unsuccessful attempts at suicide
to be the victor at Plassy and the conqueror of an
empire, than when the people of that empire lay help
less upon her conscience, and she was compelled to
listen to the demands of humanity upon her steward
ship. Her reputation suffered t less by the sharp
practices of the "John Company" and the rough riding
of her captains than by the faults and follies of her
civil administration.
The strenuous school hailed with gratification,
which the whole people came to share, the oppor
tunity of the Spanish War, and its results have made
upon the world an impression beneficial to the United
States. It has been put in sharp contrast with the
unfortunate experience of Great Britain in South
Africa, and its brevity and mighty consequences to
an ancient kingdom and once ''powerful empire have
assured to it a high place in history.
THE STRENUOUS PERIOD.
VICE PRESIDENT ROOSEVELT- made the
country familiar with his idea of the "strenu
ous* life." Â¦'. It has been held, even by some who
claim the title of philosopher, that nations must have
periods of contention, stress and warfare, lest their
people become stale. It has been argued, too, that
such periods are so fixed by natural law that they
occur, without conscious guidance or procurement,"
seeming to b* a fixed factor in national and natural
life. This country would seem to have; had wars with
sufficient frequency to keep our people in the proper
condition. From 1776 to 1858, .one hundred and
twenty-two years, we had the War of the Revolu
tion, of 1812, the Mexican War, the Civil War and
that with Spain, averaging a conspicuous war every
twenty- four years, so that each generation since the
Declaration has had a smell of powder, to say nothing
of the minor wars with our own Indians, which filled
in the gaps, and, in the case of the long struggle with
the Seminoles, the tribes led by. Tecumseh and the
Sioux, were of sufficient importance to deserve "a place
in the record of military achievement.
In a recent address before the Illinois Bar Associa
tion Judge Tuley of Chicago complained that the
people of the country districts are opposed to the city
and said that every man from the, outside counties
thinks himself "in duty bound to tie up Chicago.". If
all Chicago be like the university the country people
are nbt'tq be condemned. A sort of general tying up
or muzzling would be beneficial.
professors. cannot find time to go fishing or even to
think some original thoughts and write' addresses >q,
the multitude. So long as. salaries remained the same
the professors Would.be willing to dispense with half
their students. By what process they would deter
mine who should go; and who remain is not clear.
Some seemingly dull boys have grown to be very
learned men and some brilliant youths have done
nothing more than marry rich. Moreover, the aver
age young man and young woman in this country have
the notion that universities have been endowed for
their benefit," and they are not far wrong in the opin
ion. > It is their right to have a chance." Â«No one can
tell whether the 50-cent boy is worth a $10 education
until he hasbeen tried* T

BY UNANIMOUS VOTE CITY FRONT FEDERATION
ORDERS GENERAL STRIKE.
Continued From First Page/
The Warehousemen's Union at their
meeting last Friday night modified their
demands to 30 cents per hour, nlno hours
a day and 45 cents for overtime and Sun
days. The owners offered at the com
mencement of the strike 30 cents per hour
straight time; at a conference they of
fered 30 cents per hour and overtime after
6 p. m. at the rate of five hours per three
hours labor, but refused, to recognize the
union. As the warehousemen have never
asked for recognition of their union the
latter was easily settled. Their demands
were then modified so as to meet the own
ers half way, and as there is now only a
difference of 15 cents on nn overtime hour
a settlement will soon be reached. Secre
tary Dethlefson of the Warehousemen's
Union says:
"Since last Thursday I have expected
every moment a settlement from the own
ers. There is practically no difference be
tween us. I held five conferences last
week with the owners and modified our
demands so that an early settlement
could be reached., This morning I had a
conference with Mr. Roseman of the
Grangers' Business Association. He want
ed to settle the extra by permitting men
who did not desire to work after 5 o'clock
to quit if they so chose. I explained to
him that this would prove a bone of con
tention at all times between him and his
employes and would place him under ob
ligations to them, while on the other
hand if he granted the demands as they
stood he would be in a position to say:
'We have given you what you asked; now
you must do as we tell you or be dis
charged." He saw the logic of this and
granted the demands as they stood."
CROCKETT. July 29.â The situation of.,
the warehousemen's strike at Port Costa
looks considerably brighter at the com
mencement of its third week than at any
time heretofore. The Grangers' Ware
house this morning acceded to the de
mand of the 'employes and will resume
operations to-morro.w morning. It is
hoped that the balance of the owners will
follow the example of the Grangers' Busi
ness Association and grant the demands
of their employes within a day or two,
as there ia practically no difference be
tween them.
The submitting of the resolution was
brought about by a member of the union
who had been performing the duties' of
shipping clerk when his fellow-workmen
were locked out. He remained at work,
and through him the proprietors of the
American factory sent word to the men
that they could return to work. The men
were' willing to again pick up their tools
and resume the position they formerly
held in the shop, but will not under any
consideration arttx their signatures to an
agreement tending to disrupt the union.
They were advised to pursue this course
by President Gon! of the Labor Council,
who attended the meeting and. delivered a
speech calling upon the men to adopt con
ciliatory tactics when possible, but to re
main firm In their allegiance to their or
ganization. GofTs speech was greeted
with cheers, and when he announced that,
in his opinion, the .City Front Federation
would indorse their every action the ap
plause was tumultuous.
The membership of the Boxmakers
Union is more than 400, and. at present
every man is out.
FORT COSTA STRIKE ENDING.
The largest meeting ever held by the
Boxmakers' Union was held last night at
B'nai B'rith Hall. The men who were
locked out last Thursday all came pre
pared to discuss the troubles of the union
in their every phase, and before the meet
ing was over gave evidence that they
were prepared to meet their employers
half way. They adopted a resolution
granting- the members of the union who
formerly worked at the. American Box
Factory permission to return to work,
provided . that their former employer
would not ask them to sign any such
agreement as had been presented for their
signature during the week and would not
ask the men to perform any of the work
which was part of the work, of a team
ster.
BOXMAKERS ACT.
Two were discharged by M. Ehrman &
Co. for refusing to forsake their union
and one man was discharged by Goldstone
Brothers for the same reason. '
In discussing the strike, â¢ Robert J.
Loughery, business agent of the Porters',
Packers' and Warehousemen's Protective
Union, said:
"We are looking for peace and not for
a strike. I feel confident we have every
chance of winning in the event of an ex
tended strike. Our men are holding to
gether and will remain firm until this
lamentable trouble Is ended. At our meet
ing at the Temple last Sunday it was de
cided by a rising vote that our union
abide by the decision of the Water-Front
Federation." . , : Â¦
In consideration of your not discriminating
against, those of us who are or have been af
filiated with any labor, union, we hereby agree
to continue In your employ on the same terms
as heretofore, without any dictation from out
side organizations. â¢
Two porters were discharged at the
Yolo mills for refusing to work with non
union teamsters. â¢
Seven union and two non-union packers
were discharged by, the Armour Packing
Company yesterday morning because of
their refusal to sign an agreement which
was presented to them by the foreman
for their signatures. . The agreement
was a departure, . to a certain extent,
from the original demand of the employ
ers that the men surrender their union af
filiations, but it was considered by the
packers that the effect would be identical
and fatal to the life of the union. Tlie
agreement was worded as follows:
MUST FORSAKE UNION.
the federation were in vain. The members
of ? the union were unanimous in . wishing
that a . general strike would be ordered
and could not understand why the men of
the city front could not come to the same
conclusion without the necessity â¢ of a
lengthy session. .
Business Agent R; C. Loughery of the
Porters, Packers and Warehousemen was
attending the meeting of the delegates,
whose duty it was, according to the rules
governing organized labor, to order out
the 16,000 men employed In various 1 trades
along the. front.
The guard at the door of the union's
meeting place was the first man to re
ceive the news. When he opened the
door and announced to the anxiously
waiting laboring men that their wishes
had been granted and that on the morrow
16,000 men would quit work and enter the
ranks of the strikers and the locked-out
men the eheers were deafening. The
men yelled themselves hoarse, for the an
nouncement of the general strike meant
that their fight was to be made the fight
of every laboring man in this city.
When Business Agent Loughery arrived
at the hall he was greeted with tumultu
ous applause. He told the men of the de
liberations of, the body which had vot* J
to tie up practically all the Industries of
this city, and was cheered vociferously.
There are more than 2000 members of
the Packers', Porters' and Warehouse
men's Union. They are all engaged in the
wholesale district and their failure to re
port for work this morning will practi
cally prevent the transaction of business
in the houses in which they are employed.
While they are not looked upon as skilled
laborers, the fact remains that it will xe
quire some time to fit a green hand to
perform their duties.

NEW YORK. July 29.â The way In which the
clock market acted this morning served to dash
the hopes of the bulls among the traders, who
had Indulged a hope that some accession of
speculative interest would result from the fa
voring factors over Sunday. For two Mondays
pact the market has had to face an accumu
lation of discouraging factors which resulted in
rettllng down prices within the first hour of
trading to what proved to be the low point of
the week. This morning. In contrast, there
was the news of rains In the corn belt, about
which there waa no dispute for the first time,
as to their extent and beneficial character.
There was, besides, the new* of negotiations
and consultations between the representative*
of the t'nlted State* Steel corporation and of
the Amalgamated Association looking to the set
tlement of the strike. The opening of negotl
ctions gave strong hope of an early settlement.
The Â»lmulianfouÂ» sales of I'nltid States Steel
at thÂ« opening were of 85,000 shares at an ex
treme advance of 2 points, and a large number
Â«f the stocks thus benefited were Norfolk and
Western. Chesapeake and Ohio. Pennsylvania,
Baltimore and Ohio, New Tork Central, Fpveral
of the coalers, the Chicago and Alton Stocks,
HocVlng Valley, a number of the Southern and
Southw^Btern railroad stocks, Colorado Fuel.
American Tobacco and North American. As a
device for awakening Interest In the market
this was a failure. The opening rush to buy
was all that brought the day's dealings above
the level of activity prevailing during the lat
ter part of last week. When the" room trader*
attempted to take their profits at the end of
the day prices crumbled away and only a few
stocks had anything Jeft to show but fractional
net rains at the closing, which was dull and
heavy.
The bond market was dull and Irregular but
rather more dlverrtfled than last week. Total
sales, par value. {1.415,000.
United States bonds were all unchanged on
the last call. .-/ :. ".; . Â»

gentine, 25,000; Indian', 170,000; Australian, 48.
000. A cable from St. Petersburg reported a

tho price of corn Jumping. The American vis
ible supply, contrary to expectations, In
creased 3,583,000 bushels." .

Southwest caused a lower opening at Chi
cago, but there were plenty of buyers at tho
decline. Tho foreigners were also liberal pur
chafers. There was a large business around
68c on Wall street account. The coarse grains
were firm, and it looked a* if the soiling was
nxhaustPd. The seaboard took 75 loads for ex
port, but Chicago continued heavy, neverthe
less, with continued liquidation.

one of the pioneers of Alameda County,

at Decoto. on Saturday evening at the age
of 74 years. Mr. Crane was in business in
Alvarado when that was the county seat
of Alameda County, in 1S33. For years hÂ©
was the partner of William Whidden In a
large ranch, air! of late years he has been

cisco. The deceased wa3 the brother of A.
M. Crane, tha blind Superior Judge of
Alameda County, and leaves two sons, C.
C. Crane, the Pacific Coast agent of tha
New York Central Hne3, and H. H. Crane.
