FARMER FOXTND DEAD
IN HIS OWN TABD
August Brecht Commits Suicide Wita
Revolver on Sis Ranch ITear
Iiivermore. <
LIVERMORE. July 29.â August. Brecht,
a. farmer, 58 years of age, was found dead
in his front yard this morning with a pis
tol bullet through his head. It is undoubt
edly a case of suicide, though no cause
can be assigned for the act.
Brecht has a â¢ farm about five mile*
north of Livermore. where he has lived
for many years.- He had never married
and was known as a quiet, sober and In
dustrious man. His farm was a model
one, in which he took a great pride.
- This morning a neighbor went to the old
man's place to see him- on business and
found him dead in the yard, with a pistol
in his hand and a bullet in his brain. Tha
inquest will be held on Wednesday.
son to Judge Trask In the Superior Court
to-day, when she went upon the stand to
testify as to her treatment by her hus
band during the past few years. Mrs.
Haralson asked for a divorce from her
husband, Orr Haralson. a well-known
man about town, on the ground of exces
sive cruelty, and. after hearing her story.
Judge Trask signed the decree.
The witness said that during the time
she lived with her husband and young son
at the Abbottsf ord Inn he on one occa
sion dragged her from her bed and struck
her repeatedly until her body was black
and blue. Among other incidents of cru
elty recited by Mrs. Haralson was that
of her husband biting her finger almost
to the bone.
C. "W. Pendleton, the brother of the
plaintiff, was her attorney and offered to>
place upon the stand her young son, but
only if it was absolutely necessary. Judge
Trask waived his testimony.
"Nothing more is necessary." said his
Honor, and he signed the decree In behalf
of Mrs. Haralson.
Mrs. Haralson Is a singer of much abil
ity and is connected with prominent peo
ple in her own family. Her father, w. H.
Pendleton. was well known here as a Bap
tist preacher before his death, somsTrears
ago, and her brother is Cornelius W. Pen
dleton, Speaker of the last Assembly.

BERKELEY, July 29.â Henry Pear
son, who disappeared from West
Berkeley, Monday last, has not
been found. The police in Oak
~ n land and San FVancisco. have
searched in vain and the efforts of private
deteetives have been equally, futlte. His
wife, at her home in San Francisco, is
nearly distracted over the uncertainty of
his fate. She fears that he has . been
killed for the money he carried with him
and awaits in dread the news of the dis
covery of his body. ' ;
Pearson cannot be traced from West
Berkeley.; He visited Fruitvale a week
ago Sunday and what he did that day has
been learned. Early the next morning he
apeared in West Berkeley and borrowed
$25 from his brother-in-law, - I. A.
Crowther. The excuse upon which ho got
PROTEST FILED
AEAIHST AUTOS
the money, that of a proposed part pay
ment on a lot In Fruitvale, is hardly
counted as plausible by ' Crowther, since
he has found that Pearson left home with
nearly. $100. Crowther cannot, however,
offer any other reason. He believe3 Pear
son honest, and his actions in borrowing
the money puzzle him greatly.
Pearson's wife and friends do not believe
that he would desert his family and tha
suicide theory is not counted as probable.
He told a real estate man in Fruitvale he
would return Monday, and when he left
Crowther he said he was going there. If
he started for there how he could have
been killed and his body made away with
during the daytime is an unanswerable
question.
Pearson was an employe in a San Fran
cisco lumber mill. He was short and
heavily built, weighing between 175 and ISO
pounds. He is a Swede and has a slight
accent.

The names of W. W: Foote and William
R. Davis were presented to the City
Council to-night by the special committee
appointed to, select an attorney to assist
City Attorney Johnson in the work of
appeal in the water rates suit. The com
pensation for the lawyer who is yet to be
chosen was-fixed at $8000. Mr. Foote was
the choice of the majority of the special
committee, composed of Councilmcn
Ruch, Bon and Bishop. ' City Attorney
Johnson, who was acting with the com
mittee, presented a minority report, nam
ing Mr. Davis.
The two reports were referred, upon
motion of Councilman Courtney, to the
Ordinance and Judiciary Committee, after
Councilman Fitzgerald's motion to adopt
the minority report had been lost by the
following vote:
Ayesâ Dornin, Fitzfferald, Wallace, Wix
son; noesâ Bishop, Bon, Courtney, Ruch,
Schafer; absentâ Boyer, Cuvelller.
As soon as the majority report had been
read. City Attorney Johnson read his re
solution naming Mr. Davis, and said: "I
would very much like to have the Coun
cil act to-night on this matter, so that I
may know who shall be the attorney to
assist -me in this case. Already I have
asked for two extensions of time from
the other side and my last extension ex
pires this week. I desire that whoever
shall be chosen may Join with me at once
in starting the appeal, whether he shall
be Mr. Foote or Mr. Davis." Â»
Continuing, Mr. Johnson said Mr. Foote
was his personal friend, but that 10,000
pages of manuscript would have to be
gone through at night and Mr. Davis,
having offices in Oakland, was better sit
uated to assist in the work.
The Council adopted a resolution trans
ferring $10,000 to the general fund and $10,
000 to the high school fund. ' â¢ :
By the provisions of an ordinance intro
duced to-night the Council takes control
out of hands of the Auditor of the fund
ing of all the city's Income, holding 45 per
cent in the general fund this year for al
lotment to Other funds as the exigencies
may require.- The, only other funds for
which provision Is directly made are the
salary, police, fire and street funds. Here
tofore the funds have received their allot
ments and have been fixed at the outset
of the new fiscal year. The ordinance
went to second reading.
Councilman .Fitzgerald furnished' ihe
closing act of the session by a vigorous
attach upon,two resorts on Twelfth street,
between Broadway and Washington
streets, saying: "These places have got
to be toned down. It is , reported to me
that young girls in all stages of intoxica
tion frequent them and have been seen
going to and coming from them. The
street in front of the places is impassable.
A respectable woman cannot pass them
without fear of insult, and a man Must
fight his way through. This condition or
affairs is a disgrace and a stop must be
put to it."
The Council took no action, but Presi
dent Shaffer suggested the matter should
go before the license committee. â¢

POLICEMAN BREAKS WINDOW.
Misses the Heads of "Joshing"
Police Officer Cleveland Peters distin
guished himself yesterday morning at the
corner cf First and Brannan s?J?HÂ£ by
missing the heads of several steveabres
Â¦who were waiting to be employed on the
Mail dock and driving his club through an
expensive plate glass window in Osmer's
saloon.
Officers Peters and Owens were escorting
drays driven Jay non-union drivers. When
near the Mall dock one of the drivers
asked of the stevedores congregated there
the way to the Washington-street dock.
The men refused to give the information,
raying the drivers were "scabs." This
aroup>-d the ir.? of the officer, who was
mounted, and he drew his club and rode
into the crowd, driving the freight-hand
lers Into the numerous saloons in the
. neighborhood. The crowd was good-na
tured and chaffed the officer considerably.
Peters could net stand the "joshes" and
struck several of the men. He aimed a
blow at one of them, missed him and
smashed the large window. This aroused
the men. and angry murmurs succeeded
the "joshes," and cries of "throw the cop
per overboard" could be heard on all
sides.
Peters continued his senseless attack on
the good-natured crowd, and the affair be
gan to look serious. The officer informed
the saloon-keeper that he could "charge
the window to 'Captain Wittman," and
told the men "that they would know who
he was after he had killed a couple of
them."
The men by this time were decidedly an
gry and would have treated Peters to an
impromptu bath in the muddy water at
the foot of Brannan street had not a
prominent Mail dock official came to his
rescue and by a few words succeeded in
calming the men. Peters was then al
lowed to continue his search for Wash;
ington-street deck.
BARBERS ENJOY AN
OLD TIME IX) VE FEAST
Tonsorial Artists Hear Discussions on
Maintenance of Sanitary-
Conditions.
The Journeymen Barbers and the Mas- 1
ter Barbers held a meeting last night at
32 O'Farrell street for the purpose of dis
cussing the sanitary regulations which
were recently recommended by the State
Board of Barber Examiners. Many mem
bers of the medical profession and two
members of the Board of Health were in
attendance and delivered interesting and
instructive addresses oh how to secure,
and preserve the best sanitary conditions,
both in regard to the shop and in regard
to the tonsorial art.
At the conclusion of the meeting the
members and their guests adjourned to a
restaurant, where a delightful banquet
was enjoyed. Toasts were given and re
sponded to.
Some of the prominent people present '
and those who addressed the barbers
were: Senator E. I. Wolfe; who intro
duced the bill instituting the State Board
of Barber Examiners; J. G. Conrad, presi
dent of the Master Barbers'- Association;
Dr. J. M. Williamson, president of the
Board of Health; Dr. Baum, Dr. A. P.
O'Brien and J. G. Calish. Frank Fleming
and P. M. Drake of the State Board of
Barber Examiners.
SHIPS GO OUT INTO STREAM
Owners Whose Vessels Are Beady
for Sea Take Precautionary
Measure.
The shipowners evidently feared a gen
eral strike yesterday, as all who had
their vessel? anywhere near ready for sea
cleared them at the Custom-house and
got them out in the stream. The ship
Emily F. Whitney was brought over from
Oakland, the g. T. Alexander was taken
away from Harrison and the Gerard C.
Tobey from the seawall, while the col
lier Wellington is ready to go to sea at
a moments notice.
As soon as the orders "to drop into the
Ft roam" reached the vessels several of
tho Failcrs rushed up to the office of the
Stamen's l"nSon on the Pacific and asked
Secretary Furuseth what they should do.
"No strike has been ordered as yet
Stick to your shipn," was the secretary's
instruction.

CHINAMAN WHO CROSSED
THE OCEAN TO SECURE RE
VENGE BY MURDER.
While the horse was drinking a big dog
came running madly down . the road and
Jumped into the trough. It performed
this feat so suddenly that the horse be
came frightened and started to run. Mrs.
Merwin lost her presence of mind imme
diately, making no attempt to grasp the
reins, which were within easy reach of
her. Having nothing to guide it the horse
ran off the road and up the side of a
steep hill. Below the road was a. deep
gulch. To tip over meant death to Mrs.
Merwin, as there could be no stopping
until the bottom of the gulch was reached.
Just as it seemed certain that the cart
would- turn turtle Mrs. Pawling came to
the rescue. She had been walking along
the road with some friends and had seen
the runaway. She is large and muscular
and intrepid, and when she saw Mrs. Mer
win's peril rushed to her aid.
Bracing herself against a small rock
that stuck out of the hillside Mrs. Pawl
ing grabbed hold of- a wheel of the top
pling cart on the side which was lowest
and hold tight, at the same time crying to
Mrs. Merwin to stop the horse. The
frightened woman did as she was bidden,
and in a moment the danger was over.
Mrs. Pawling held the cart until the oc
cupant got out and then was assisted in
getting it to the road by others of the
party.
Mrs. Pawling looks upon her brave act
as nothing extraordinary. She lives with
her husband at 3212 Encinal avenue.
Mrs. Merwin and a woman friend were
driving along one of the high grades In
the neighborhood of Larkspur just pre
vious to the occurrence of the accident
that came so near terminating fatally.
They had stopped at a trough to
water the horse, Mrs. Merwin remaining
In the vehicle while her companion alight
ed for the purpose of loosing the check
rein.
ALAMEDA, July 23.â Risking her own
life, and by main strength In a moment
of peril, Mrs. Frederick F. Pawling of
Alameda saved Mrs. William MerWih from
death or serious injury at Larkspur yes
terday afternoon. Just as Mrs. Merwin
was about to be thrown- out of a cart
down a steep niilside Mrs. Pawling caught
hold of the vehicle and held it until the
frightened occupant Was able to make
her escape. If she had not had the
strength to hold the cart in place its
weight would have carried both the wom
en to the bottom of a deep gulch.
Mrs. P. F. Pawling Goes
to Rescue at Moment
of Peril.
RISKS HER LIFE
TO STOP RUNAWAY
JUE DO HONG, a Chinese boy, 15
years of age, was fatally shot in
the back at S2S Washington street
yesterday morning 1 by Jue Lin Ong,
who has so far succeeded in escap
ing the vigilance of the police. The mur
dered boy lived with his uncle, Jue Woh
Kit, at the place where the shooting oc
curred. Two shots were fired into the
boy's back. He was taken to the Receiv
ing Hospital, where he made an ante
mortem statement to Detective Bailey,
accusing: Jue Lin Ong of having shot him.
The boy died about 2 o'clock and his body
was removed to the Morgue.
There was trouble about property be
tween the boy's father in China and the
murderer, who arrived here from China
about four months ago to wreak his
vengeance on the boy. He remained here
about two months and left the city, re
turning Sunday night. He borrowed con
siderable money from friends in China
town and had his personal effects ready
packed to be taken away. He met the
boy in the basement of 828 Washington
street about 7 o'clock yesterday morning
and asked him if he would go with him
to locate fomc friends. As the boy turned
to go with him he fired two shots into
his back.
Stephen Dowd. an electrician, living at
1811& Mason street, was passing at the
time and chased the murderer for some
minutes, but was unsuccessful in catch
ing him. Dowd returned and picked up
the revolver, a 44-caliber Colt's, and turn-

PAGING EVENTS
ON OHIO TRACK
was assassinated at Monza July 29 last by
Gastani Bresci, an anarchist) was com
memorated to-day by services throughout
Italy. Rome is the center of the observ
ances and there are 300,000 visitors here on
a pilgrimage to the tomb of King; Hum
bert, in the Pantheon. In addition, mem
bers of the royal family. Senators and
Deputies assembled at the capitol to take
part in the ceremonies.
The ceremonies commenced at 7 o'clock
this morning, when the King, Queen Hel
ena, Queen Margarita and Queen Maria
Pia (Dowager Queen of Portugal and a
princess of the house of Savoy) and the
princes and princesses prayed at the tomb
of the late King. The royal party after
ward attended mass. At 10 o'clock high
mass was celebrated, in the Pantheon,
which was imposingly decorated inside
and outside. Very conspicuous in the
oenter of the edifice stood a catafalque,
surmounted by the royal insignia and sur
rounded by innumerable wax tapers. \ De
tachments of royal cuirassiers were posted
about the building and other troops cor
doned the Pantheon Square.
Representatives of the King. the
Knights of the Order of the Annunziata,
the Cabinet Ministers, the diplomatic
corps, the state and provincial authorities,
the Senators and Deputies and many
naval and military officers were present at
the high mass.
The pilgrimage to the Pantheon occurred
at 1 o'clock in the afternoon. There was
an immense procession composed of dele
gations from all the Italian provinces and
colonies, and civil, military and labor as
sociations, with flags and bands of music.
It was estimated that over 100,000 persons
were in the parade. The most picturesque
participants were garbed in multi-colored
historic costumes. Innumerable wreaths
were carried in the procession. Enormous
crowds lined the route and the windows,
balconies and roofs were crowded to their
utmost capacity. All stores were closed
- and everywhere flags were displayed at
half-mast. As the procession entered the
Pantheon those who participated in It filed
past the vault containing the remains of
King Humbert and there deposited the
wreaths.
The ceremony will close Tuesday at the
Adriano Theater, where addresses will be
delivered.
The most noteworthy provincial celebra
tion occurred at Milan, in which city
there was a pilgrimage to Monza. the
place where King Humbert was assassin
ated. The wreaths were so numerous that
the field on which King Humbert's last
review was held was converted into a
flower garden. The first stone of the ex
pi ory chapel which is being erected by
K' g Victor -Emmanuel was laid by the
Du..e of Abruzzi, representing the King of
Italy.

to Rome and Attend.
Mass in Pantheon.

This statement was signed by Edward
J. White, a British soldier; Alme Van
Den Bogaerda, a Belgian, and Charles F.
Pope, a citizen of the United States.
Is either the officers of the Allegheny
nor officials of the line would make any
statement concerning the arrest of Mur
rillo. ;-. . -.-'.-.
"The captain then went on land and
after a while returned with more police
men, and we then, thought that he was
.goin^ to give up the passenger in ex
change for his papers. The police went
up. to Murrillo and tearing from him the
â¢dirty rag," as they called the flag of
Kaiser Wilhelm, took the prisoner from
the ship."
The anest did not take place at this
time, and the police withdrew on the cap
tain's, protest. Later, however, the ship's
clearance papeis were refused, and the
statement was made that they would not
be furnished until Murrillo was surren
dered. A signed statement concerning the
incident then says:
According 1 to a signed statement made
by three of the Allegheny's passengers
MHrrillo left the United States about four
months ayo on a passport signed by the
Colombian Minister at Washington. On
hia arrival at Savanilla he was arrested
and taken to Pogoto, where he waa re
leased on the understanding that he would
sail on the first vessel for the United
States. This Murrillo did, boarding the-
Alleifheny at Savanilla. He expressed
fears that he would be arrested at Carta
gena, and when the vessel arrived at that
port he refused to go ashore when word
was brought that the Governor wanted
to see him. His arrest followed. The
statement made by the passengers there
says that Captain Lowe of the Allegheny
protested against the arrest, saying it was
against International law, and at the same
time the captain told the mate to put the
German flag on the gangway so that thÂ»
Colombian officers would have to tread on
U If they took Murrillo.
According 1 to the passengers on the Al
legheny the Colombian officers, notwith
standing the protest, seized the man and
dragged him from the vessel.
"I am under the protection of the Ger
man flag and you have no right to arrest
me."'
When the vessel arrived at Cartagena
she was ordered detained by the authori
ties there. The captain protested that he
was sailing under the German flag and
that no official of Colombia had a right
to stop the vessel for any purpose what
ever. The protest was unheeded, how
ever, and search was made for Murrillo,
who was found on deck. He declared that
he would not be arrested, and running to
one of the ship's masts he seized the Ger
man flag, which was lyingr there,, and
wrapped it about him. Then he * ! ?od for
ward and cried out:
NEW YORK, July Â».-The Hamburg-
American line steamer Allegheny, which
arrived here to-day, reported that she
was held In the harbor of Savanilla. Co
lombia, for twelve hours. Passengers on
the Allegheny report that Abel Murrlllo
was arrested on the ship at Cartagena
and was taken ashore by the Colombian
authorities. Murrillo protested against his
arrest, claiming that he was entitled to
the protection of the German flag. Mur
rillo is said to be the secretary of Gen
eral Uribe.

Thursday evening" last was a gala one
for the members of the San Francisco
Scottish Thistle Club. The games com
mittee presented Its final report, which
was enthusiastically received. To cele
brate the success achieved upon that oc
casion it was decided to suspend business
and celebrate in an informal manner. Ac
cordingly refreshments were procured and
the rest of the evening was given up to
pleasure. An impromptu programme, con
sisting of Instrumental solos, songs, reci
tations and speeches, was arranged and
proved highly entertaining.
The affair was voted the most success
ful in the history of the club. The fol
lowing named clansmen and friends con
tributed toward the evening's pleasure:
A. Strang, J. Davidson. Thomas Nixon,
G. W. Paterson. A. A. Brodie, D. Donald,
A. Nicholson, G. Fullerton, H. Fraser, J.
C Moore, J. V. McKay, J. Poniton G
Miller, P. D. Findlay and W. Murray.

Opal Morris, a pretty, blue-eyed child
of 8 years, pleaded on bended knees be
fore Judge Dunne yesterday morning to
be permitted to remain with Mrs. Schwab
at -11 Julian avenue, who has cared* for
her since last May, when her mother,
Magpie Morris, died. The child's father.
Guy Morris, disappeared about two years
agj and the mother was forced to support
herself and child.
Finally Mrs. Morris was taken ill and
died. James W. Barnetr of Stockton, the
child's grandfather, recently applied for
letter? of guardianship upon the person
and estate of his little granddaughter.
The petition for letters came up before
Judgt- Dunne yesterday. The child rose
painfully from her seat, being affected
with spinal trouble, and pleaded in a pit
eous strain that she might be left witn
Mrs. Schwab, whom 6he .called her
mothc-r.
Suddenly the little girl dropped to her
knecp and with uplifted hands implored
the court to permit her to remain with
"he*- mother."
All m the courtroom were visibly affect
ed, but Judge Dunne had no alternative
but to grant the petition for letters. An

month or two. and in all probability she

Besides his wife he leaves two daugh
ters and two sons, one of whom, William
.E. Mills, is living at his father's old home
stead in Quincy. Piumas County.
The late Professor Le Conte, of whom
he was a close friend, was a student at
Harvard at aDout the same time as Mr.
Mills and took the degrees in the same
school of science. Both had the same
epiritual nature, and science only seemed
to intensify their love of God and rever
ence for his works. Professor Davidson
was also intimately acquainted with Mr.
Mills, cf whose work he could doubtless
tell much that would be of Interest.
Alexander Agassiz one day remarked to
Mr. Mills that his father had said that
the old water courses of the Sierras cross
each other at Ftrange angles, and that
there would be found the most Interesting
geological formation on the face of the
earth, and that Mills was the one man
to investigate it and solve the problem.
About twenty years ago he established a
geological survey at his own expense, and
the result of his labors are maps show-
Ing in detail ani accurately the formation
of the Sierras, the changes of the water
courses and ths causes thereof. He deliv
ered an address on the subject before the
Geological Society in this city several
years ago.
Deceased was born at Bangor, Me., Feb
ruary 13, 3834. He was a son of Dr. P. B.
Mills, a prominent physician, and a broth
er of Hiram F. Mills, the hydraulic en
gineer and expert in sewerage, than whom
there is not a l.Â« tter known man in scien
tific circles :n Massachusetts. When
IS years old James E. Mills went to Bos
ton and entered the Lawrence Scientific
School of Harverd. over which the elder
Agasslz presided. He took the degree of
S. B. six years later, and in the same
year was ordaired a minister of the Swe
denborgian church. He occupied a pulpit
in Brooklyn for three years, when com
pelled to resign because of failing health.
He became a mining geologist and was
soon engaged in most important work for
the elder Agassiz, and after his death for
his son. Alexander Agassiz, in whose em
ploy he was at the time of his death.
James E. Mills, the noted geologist, who
was a classmate and life-long friend of
the l&te Professor Joseph Le Conte,
passed away last Saturday at Culiacan,
Mexico. The sad news was received yes
terday by James H. Barry, but no details
as to the cause of death were given.

A meeting of the Election Commission
ers was held yesterday at which the proc
lamation calling for the primary election
to be held on- Tuesday,' August 13, was
adopted. Nineteen persons. were cited to
appear before the board next Monday to
show cause "why their names should not
be expunged from the register. A ean
vass of the Twenty-eighth, Twenty-ninth,
Forty-third and Forty-fifth districts de
veloped the fact that many persons had
given fictitious residences and that others
had moved since they, last registered. -
i Registrar, Walsh announced that the
registration for the primary election
would close at 11 p. m. next Saturday.
Up to that date the registration office
will remain open until 9?30 p. m. daily.
The board ordered the sending of a re
quisition (or thirty clerks to the Civil Ser
vice. Commissioners, that number of as
sistants being required- for the perform
ance of the extra work attending- the pri
mary registration and election. ,

Interesting Papers Axe Bead at Eacli

erings. Â»
A most Interesting paper was read yes
terday by Rev. Dr. E. H. Avery at the
weekly meeting of the Presbyterian Min
isterial Union, the subject Â«f the paper
being "The Bible, What Is It?"
In presenting theyaddress, the speaker
dwelt upon six mest important facts,
namely; That the Bible furnishes a mass
of highly important historical data, un
obtainable elsewhere in literature; that
the work extending through fifteen cen
turies shows a remarkable harmony of
thought; that the writings present a code
of laws, perfect and unapproachable: that
the Scriptures present a knowledge of
events still deep in the future; that the
writers distinctly claim divine guidance
and that the Savior recoRniaed the Old
Testament as the word of God. A. dis
cussion folio-wed th# reading of the paper.
At the weekly meeting of the Methodist
Church Union an address was delivered
by the Rev. Dr. Thirkield, corresponding
secretary of the Freedmen's v Aid and
Southern Educational Society. The sub
ject of the address, was "On the Relation
of the Negro Race to the Conversion of
the World." The speaker handled the
subject in a forceful manner and was list
ened to with marked attention. Regular
meetings were also held yesterday by the
pastors of the Baptist and Congregational
churches. .

VINCENT BIASIOLI, lj? yeafs old
and very much in love with; Miss
Fannie Â¦ Rousseau, occupies & Â¦ cell |
in the Hall of Justice with an
ugly charge of arson booked
against him. Vincent made a very raw
attempt early yesterday -morning to- set
fire to a flat occupied, by him at 425 Bart
iett street, his object being to collect the
$200 for which the place was" insured and
thereby be financially in a position to wed
the object of his affections.
Biasioli went about the business of set
ting fire to his flat in a very amateurish
fashion. About 1 o'clock he poured a can
of coal oil all over the.basement floor and
took some coal oil-soaked rags and hung
them alcng the walls. Then he put a
match to the rags and waited for the
place to burn. Just as the fire was gain
ing headway Mr. Kane, who lives in the
adjoining house, came up the street and,
seeing- the flames,' proceeded to call for
assistance. Biasioli came out in .answer
to his cries and helped him to fight the
fire. To Kane the youth explained that
the fire must have been the work of burg
lars.
Shortly after 5 o'clock Biasioli made an
other attempt to fire the house. This time
he chose the parlor and bedroom of his
flat as* the scene of action and poured coal
oil over the bedsheets and on the cur
tains'that adorned the parlor windows.
When the flames began to spread the
young incendiary went out and roused his
At first Biasioli absolutely refused to
speak of the lady for the sake of whose
love he sinned. "She has nothing to do
with it." he said. "I alone am to blame.
If she had knov/n that I was going to do
what I did F am sure she -would have told
me not to, â¢ s
"How shWl I ever face her people?" he
went on. ' "I hope she will forgive" me. I
Young Biasioli is a comely youth, just
turned 19. His skin is fair and his cheeks
pink, just like a girl's. His hair is brown
and curly, and his large brown eyes fill
and solash over when he tellg the story
of his crime, just like a child's.
"I do not know what possessed me to do
such a thing," said the youth. "The idea
came into my head on Friday night when
I heard the lire bells ring. I am engaged
to be married, and I thought if I had the
$200 for which my flat was insured I might
be wedded right away.
"I never for a moment realized what an
awful crime I was committing," went on
the youth, sobbing as if his heart would
break. "I never did anything wrong In
my life before, and I hope the law will
take pity on me and give me another
chance." -*â¢'Â« â¢'Â»Â£â¢-

. Miss Rousseau is a remarkably self-pos

er's sad plight. She had evidently thought

She is very pretty, this girl who made

lar,-her eyes blue,' her hair heavy and of
a deep nut brown and worn in two braids.
Marguerite. fashion, down her back. She
is 19 years old.

When he rented the house he purchased

A meeting of the board of directors. of
the California Cricket Association was
held last night, at which delegates were
present from the Alameda, California, Pa
cific and Santa Cruz clubs. An amend
ment to the constitution was passed to the
effect that delegates from any club to the
association shall not be permitted to , act
as members of the board of directors un
til notice of their appointment shall have
been communicated by the secretary of
their club to the secretarypf .the associa
tion.
E. G. Sloman. one of the delegates of
the California Cricket Club, introduced a
motion that the Alameda Club, having in
cluded in its team a player who left the
Californias when not in good standing,
forfeit all its matches for the pennant
played during the present season. After a
long and vigorous discussion, in which
all the delegates took part, the motion
was withdrawn. . . _ - Â«
President Edward Brown appointed H.
C. Casidy, R. B. Hogue and A. G. Sheath
a committee of three to select a team to
represent Old England in the match next
Sunday, and also to visit Lake County
during the Admission day holidays. The
Young America team to play next Sunday
against Old England will be picked by F.
J. Croll, i assisted by two committeemen
selected, by himself.
Owing to the lateness of the hour a
communication from A. E. Acklom with
reference to procuring a permanent cricket
ground and erecting . suitable . buildings
thereon Â¦was deferred until the next meet
ing. .

The resolutions to be adopted will also
deal -with the diversion of repair work
for the Mare Island yard to Port Orchard
and to the ship yards of the Atlantic
coast. The merchants who .went to Mare
Island were stFongly in favor of vigor
ously calling this matter ; to the attention
of Congress. There is no line of business
in San Francisco that is not interested
financially in having times brisk at Mare
.Island, for all the supplies, as Secretary
Campbell of the "Vallejo Board of Trade
said, in addressing the merchants, come
from San Francisco. The meeting on the
General Frisbie was informal and that
was the reason that no action was taken
during the trip. The course adopted by
the San Francisco Board of Trade will re
sult in having all the influence that can
be secured back of the action of the com
mercial bodies of the city. Â¦.-â¢ . '
. The general suggestions that were con
tained in these resolutions , will be the
basis of action by the San Francisco
Board of Trade.' They recommend the
immediate construction of the stone dry
dock at the yard and also 'the deepening
of the water over the San Pablo flat, that
warships may find no difficulty in going
to. the yard freely. The contract for the
dry dock only awaits the signature of the
Secretary of the Navy, having been pre
pared under the direction of a naval board
and having been accepted as entirely sat
isfactory by the contractors, who .are
ready to go to work at once. The other
project, the deepening of the water, has
been recommended by Colonel Heuer.
The Board of Trade of San Francisco
will take up the matter of the necessary 1
improvements for the Mare Island navy
yard at a meeting which has* been called
by Preeident-A. A. Watklns, to be held on
Thursday of the present week. The sup
position is that the board will adopt reso
lutions which will be subsequently sub
mitted to the Chamber oÂ£ - Commerce,
Manufacturers' and Producers'- Associa
tion, Merchants' Association, San Fran
cisco Produce Exchange and the newly
organized Merchants' Exchange for in
dorsement. "When the merchants of San
Francisco recently went to Mare Island to
inspect the yard and to be Informed con
cerning the work that needs to be done to
provide the best facilities for the ( build
ing and repairing of ships' of the navy,
resolutions had been prepared at the in
stance of the committee, representing the
Vallejo Board of Trade and these were
handed to President Watklns, who presid
ed at the informal -meetings . that were
held on the steamer General \Frisbie
while the merchants were journeying to
and from the yard. . -
